Data and Methods

Data

Metadata
Query TS = ((“nature” OR “natural” OR “environmental” OR “green” OR “sustainab” OR ”climate” OR ”biodiversity” OR ”transition” OR ”resilience” OR ”adaptation”) NEAR/1 (”financ” OR “investment” OR ”disclosure” OR ”credit” OR “bank” OR “fund” OR “capital” OR ”asset”)) OR TS = (“green bond” OR ”sustainable bond” OR “sustainability bond*” OR “natural capital” )
Database WOS
Documents 44082
Date retrieved 2024-08-08
Fukan Analysis Not apply.
ID Q300

Methods

  • Data collection from WOS
  • Created citation network
  • Extracted Maximum Component
  • Clustering using the Louvain method
  • Cluster description

Cluster 1-1—: Corporate Environmental Disclosure and Performance

Documents: 702; Ave. Year: 2020.1; Ave. Citations: 26.6
The texts collectively explore the complex interplay between corporate environmental information disclosure, environmental performance, and economic outcomes. By analyzing empirical data and models, the studies shed light on how the level and quality of environmental disclosures by companies, particularly in industries like mining, are influenced by various factors such as board attributes, employee stock ownership, and regulatory systems. The research also examines the impact of these disclosures on metrics of environmental performance like energy efficiency and pollution control, as well as on the financial performance of the companies. The findings highlight the role of effective corporate governance mechanisms in driving environmental engagement and transparency.

Articles: The study examines the relationship between environmental accounting information disclosure, environmental performance, board attributes, and profitability of Chinese mining companies, finding a positive association between environmental disclosures, performance, and firm profitability from the nature capital perspective. (Agyemang et al. 2024) degree: 15 citations: 15
Environmental information disclosure mandated in China in 2008 significantly improved energy efficiency by promoting industrial upgrading and technological innovation, with public participation playing a key role. (LH. Wang and Shao 2024) degree: 7 citations: 7
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems and promote sustainable development, aligning environmental performance with economic performance through quantifiable environmental disclosures. (Al-Tuwaijri, Christensen, and Hughes 2004) degree: 1186 citations: 1186
Corporate social responsibility reporting in the mining industry has progressed, with varying levels of sophistication and a clear divide between leaders and laggards. (Jenkins and Yakovleva 2006) degree: 610 citations: 610
Corporate environmental information disclosure in Chinese listed companies is significantly influenced by government pressure and company size, while the role of shareholders and creditors is still weak, with selective disclosure based on region and economic performance. (XB. Liu and Anbumozhi 2009) degree: 482 citations: 482
ESOPs granted to non-executive employees promote greater corporate ecological engagement through increased environmental expenditures, disclosure, and ratings by aligning employee interests with long-term firm value creation. (Kong et al. 2024) degree: 31 citations: 31

Cluster 1-2—: Corporate Sustainability and Financial Performance

Documents: 654; Ave. Year: 2021.2; Ave. Citations: 22.6
The cluster of texts explores the complex interplay between corporate sustainability practices, particularly environmental, social, and governance (ESG) disclosures, and firm financial performance. The literature suggests that companies’ commitment to sustainability, as evidenced by their ESG disclosures and practices, can positively influence their financial outcomes. Environmental and social performance, along with comprehensive sustainability reporting, are found to enhance profitability and long-term financial stability. However, the impact of governance disclosures on financial performance remains inconclusive. The relationship between ESG factors and corporate financial success is further moderated by variables such as ESG ratings, ownership structure, firm size, and industry. Ultimately, the texts highlight that prioritizing sustainability, especially in the environmental and social domains, can bolster a firm’s reputation, mitigate information asymmetry, and contribute to its long-term financial well-being.

Articles: The study finds that environmental and social disclosures positively impact financial performance of energy firms in Belt and Road Initiative countries, with ownership concentration strengthening this relationship. (DJ. Zhou, Saeed, and Agyemang 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
Nature Capital refers to investing in or financing the protection and restoration of the planet’s natural ecosystems, biodiversity conservation and sustainable development using economic incentives and financial tools. (Moufty, Al-Najjar, and Ibrahim 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
Good stakeholder relations help poorly performing firms recover faster from disadvantageous positions and enable superior firms to sustain competitive advantage longer. (Choi and Wang 2009) degree: 523 citations: 523
The study examines the influence of firm size and availability of resources on ESG (environmental, social, and governance) scores provided by rating agencies, raising concerns about whether these scores accurately measure corporate sustainability and serve the needs of socially responsible investors. (Drempetic, Klein, and Zwergel 2020) degree: 363 citations: 363
This study examines the inverse association between corporate social responsibility (CSR) engagement and information asymmetry, supporting the stakeholder theory that CSR acts as a reputation-building vehicle, enhancing the firm’s information environment. (Cui, Jo, and Na 2018) degree: 366 citations: 366
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives, financial tools, and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and drive sustainable development. (Ahmad, Yaqub, and Lee 2024) degree: 25 citations: 25

Cluster 1-3—: Environmental Sustainability and Firm Performance

Documents: 638; Ave. Year: 2018.8; Ave. Citations: 38
The texts collectively investigate the impact of environmental sustainability practices, such as green management, carbon reduction initiatives, and strong environmental performance, on firm financial performance and resilience, especially in the context of crises like the COVID-19 pandemic. The studies generally find a positive association between adopting environmentally sustainable measures and improved financial outcomes, market valuations, and the ability to withstand economic shocks. However, the relationship is nuanced, with factors like corporate governance, CSR strategies, and industry characteristics playing a moderating role. The findings suggest that investing in environmental sustainability can provide firms with competitive advantages and enhance their resilience to unexpected shocks, highlighting the importance of policies and incentives that encourage the adoption of such practices.

Articles: Firms with better environmental practices were more resilient to the COVID-19 crisis, while prior difficulties in accessing credit exacerbated the pandemic’s negative impact on businesses’ performance and financing. (Aristei and Gallo 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
Family firms exhibited substantially higher financial performance than non-family firms during the COVID-19 pandemic, suggesting their greater resilience in crises. (Miroshnychenko et al. 2024) degree: 4 citations: 4
Empirical analysis finds a positive link between carbon emissions reduction initiatives and financial performance, with corporate social responsibility strategy and strong governance further enhancing this relationship, especially after the Paris Agreement. (Issa 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
Empirical evidence shows a positive link between strong environmental management practices and improved financial performance as measured by stock market valuation. (Klassen and McLaughlin 1996) degree: 1683 citations: 1683
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and achieve sustainable development. (Ambec and Lanoie 2008) degree: 1097 citations: 1097
Despite values promoting diversity, the lack of consensus in organizational science hinders its scientific progress and ability to compete for resources with more paradigmatically developed fields. (PFEFFER 1993) degree: 897 citations: 897

Cluster 1-4—: Environmental Disclosures and Sustainability Performance

Documents: 594; Ave. Year: 2019.1; Ave. Citations: 41.7
The cluster of texts explores the complex interplay between corporate environmental disclosures, sustainability performance, and financial reporting practices. Researchers investigate the motivations behind environmental disclosures, drawing on legitimacy theory and economics-based disclosure theories, and examine the relationship between these disclosures and actual environmental performance. Evidence suggests that firms with poorer environmental records tend to provide more positive disclosures to manage public perceptions, using environmental reporting as a legitimizing tool. The studies also highlight the impact of corporate governance mechanisms, such as board diversity, on mitigating the negative effects of earnings management on environmental, social, and governance (ESG) performance. Furthermore, the role of renewable and non-renewable energy in achieving sustainable development goals in South Asia is emphasized, underscoring the importance of energy in the broader context of sustainability.

Articles: Earnings management negatively impacts environmental, social and governance (ESG) performance, but strong corporate governance mechanisms like board gender diversity can mitigate this negative effect by reducing managerial resource exploitation for unsustainable investments. (Adeneye et al. 2024) degree: 6 citations: 6
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives, financial tools, and investments to protect and restore ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and support sustainable development. (Bilal. et al. 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
This study examines the relationship between corporate environmental performance and discretionary environmental disclosures, finding a positive association consistent with economic disclosure theory but also evidence supporting socio-political theories. (Clarkson et al. 2008) degree: 1760 citations: 1760
Companies use environmental disclosures as a legitimizing tool to offset poor environmental performance, with disclosure patterns varying based on industry sensitivity and performance levels. (Cho and Patten 2007) degree: 1109 citations: 1109
Environmental disclosures in annual reports are examined to understand their role in managing public impressions, focusing on the influence of external pressure, their characteristics compared to other social disclosures, and the association between disclosures and actual environmental performance. (Neu, Warsame, and Pedwell 1998) degree: 961 citations: 961
Renewable and non-renewable energies have significant positive long-term impacts on sustainable development in South Asia, according to an econometric study using panel data from 1995-2019. (Noor et al. 2024) degree: 10 citations: 10

Cluster 1-5—: Corporate Sustainability Reporting and Materiality

Documents: 379; Ave. Year: 2020.2; Ave. Citations: 26.8
The cluster of texts collectively examines the quality, credibility, and materiality of corporate sustainability reporting and disclosure practices, with a particular focus on the concept of double materiality. This approach considers both the financial impacts on the company and the company’s impacts on the environment and society. The studies assess the consistency between companies’ claims of applying double materiality and their actual compliance, the adequacy of climate risk disclosure, and the relationship between integrated reporting quality and sustainability performance. Furthermore, the texts explore factors influencing voluntary assurance of sustainability reports, the gap between sustainability talk and practice, and the value implications of material versus immaterial sustainability investments. Ultimately, these articles contribute to the broader discussion of evaluating the materiality and credibility of corporate sustainability reporting and disclosure.

Articles: European companies claimed to apply double materiality in sustainability reporting, but 67% did not comply with the guidelines, indicating they were merely “label adopters” without substantive changes to their material issues. (Correa-Mej’ıa, Correa-Garc’ıa, and Garc’ıa-Benau 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
This study evaluates the adequacy of corporate climate risk disclosure and argues for redefining materiality from a broader value creation perspective that includes sustainable development impact, using a novel model based on GRI principles. (Arian and Sands 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
Integrated reporting quality positively impacts sustainability performance, but this relationship is weaker for environmentally sensitive firms, suggesting they may disclose less sustainability information under mandatory integrated reporting regimes. (Appiagyei and Donkor 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
Nature Capital investments aim to protect ecosystems and biodiversity through economic incentives and financial tools. (Simnett, Vanstraelen, and Chua 2009) degree: 801 citations: 801
Organized hypocrisy and organizational facades limit the potential for corporate sustainability reporting to drive meaningful change. (Cho et al. 2015) degree: 496 citations: 496
Firms investing in sustainability issues material to their industry outperform those neglecting such issues, while immaterial sustainability investments show no performance impact. (M. Khan, Serafeim, and Yoon 2016) degree: 444 citations: 444

Cluster 1-6—: Corporate Governance and Environmental Disclosure

Documents: 154; Ave. Year: 2019.8; Ave. Citations: 30.4
The texts collectively examine the impact of various corporate governance factors on the environmental disclosure practices and sustainability reporting of companies. These factors include board composition, such as independence and gender diversity, CEO characteristics, and institutional ownership. The studies explore how elements like board independence, female representation, CEO traits, and institutional investor pressure influence the extent, quality, and transparency of environmental and corporate social responsibility (CSR) disclosures. Additionally, some studies investigate the role of media coverage and cross-country differences in shaping these disclosure practices.

Articles: The systematic literature review examines the understudied relationship between CEO characteristics and corporate environmental sustainability, identifying gaps and opportunities for future research on CEO psychological traits, environmental disclosure aspects, and cross-country/industry differences. (Mahran and Elamer 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
Focusing on Nature Capital, this study investigates how institutional investors from different countries and listing statuses influence environmental disclosure by Indonesian companies, promoting sustainable investing and biodiversity conservation. (Wicaksono et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
The study found that boards with more independent directors, separate CEO and chairperson roles, and higher overall effectiveness positively influenced environmental disclosures by Indian companies, while gender diversity had an insignificant impact. (Vig 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
Nature Capital investing focuses on using economic incentives and financial tools to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and support sustainable development. (Fifka 2013) degree: 376 citations: 376
Board gender diversity and independence positively influence firms’ corporate social responsibility disclosure, ethical policy establishment, and resource allocation towards sustainable development initiatives. (Jizi 2017) degree: 260 citations: 260
Examining governance, media coverage, and board attributes on voluntary environmental disclosure quality for 127 firms from 2000-2005, the study found positive associations with environmental media, negative media, board independence, diversity, expertise, and disclosure quality increasing over time. (Rupley, Brown, and Marshall 2012) degree: 251 citations: 251

Cluster 1-7—: Corporate climate change disclosure

Documents: 127; Ave. Year: 2020.9; Ave. Citations: 20.6
The cluster of papers examines corporate climate change disclosure and reporting practices, focusing on the factors that drive firms to voluntarily disclose climate-related information and the implications of such disclosures. Researchers investigate the relationship between corporate governance elements, such as board effectiveness, gender diversity, and institutional investor engagement, and companies’ willingness to report on climate change risks. The materiality and quality of climate risk disclosures in regulatory filings are analyzed to understand their impact on firm risk measures, including the cost of equity. Some studies explore how companies in specific industries, like mining, use climate change disclosure as a legitimacy-seeking strategy to manage stakeholder perceptions. A bibliometric analysis identifies major research themes surrounding corporate environmental strategies, reporting, governance, and finance in the context of climate change. Overall, the papers in this cluster aim to shed light on the practices, determinants, and consequences of corporate climate change disclosure and reporting, framed through the lens of environmental accountability.

Articles: Board effectiveness positively influences firms’ voluntary disclosure of climate change information through responding to the Carbon Disclosure Project questionnaire and enhancing disclosure quality on climate risks and mitigation strategies. (Ben-Amar and McIlkenny 2015) degree: 163 citations: 163
Firms disclosing climate risk in 10-K filings have lower cost of equity, especially in industries where climate risk is expected to be material, suggesting markets consider climate risk materiality when assessing disclosure credibility. (Matsumura, Prakash, and Vera-Muñoz 2024) degree: 12 citations: 12
The study explores how Australian mining companies and industry bodies use disclosure strategies across various media to respond to climate change and maintain their social license to operate, employing a combination of legitimizing tactics to varying degrees based on their stakeholder focus. (Pellegrino and Lodhia 2012) degree: 126 citations: 126
Board gender diversity positively impacts firms’ likelihood of voluntarily disclosing climate change-related risks and greenhouse gas emissions, supporting stakeholders’ demands for environmental transparency and sustainability initiatives. (Ben-Amar, Chang, and McIlkenny 2017) degree: 498 citations: 498
Institutional investors’ site visits significantly promote corporate disclosure of climate change risks among Chinese listed companies. (YH. Song and Xian 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
This bibliometric analysis on climate change and organizational research from 1999-2021 identifies five key thematic clusters, with environmental finance representing an understudied area requiring further attention from a nature capital perspective. (Tautiva, Huaman, and Oliva 2024) degree: 6 citations: 6

Cluster 1-99—: Organizational Sustainability

Documents: 138; Ave. Year: 2019; Ave. Citations: 27.1
The texts collectively explore the complex interplay between sustainability and financial performance in organizations, highlighting the need to strike a balance between environmental, social, and governance (ESG) goals and long-term financial viability. They emphasize the importance of adopting sustainable practices, such as sustainable procurement, circular innovations, and comprehensive sustainability strategies, while also addressing the challenges and opportunities associated with these initiatives. The articles shed light on the potential barriers and enablers for implementing sustainable practices, as well as the benefits that organizations can reap, including improved resource efficiency, waste reduction, and long-term financial gains. Ultimately, the texts underscore the critical role that organizations play in driving the transition towards a more sustainable future while ensuring their own financial sustainability and profitability.

Articles: Nature capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and drive sustainable development. (Rametsteiner et al. 2011) degree: 211 citations: 211
State-owned enterprises in South Africa balance environmental, social, governance practices with financial sustainability, though there is no systematic approach, leading to conflicts between these objectives. (A. Adebayo and Ackers 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Australian local governments face barriers like poor finances, lack of training, and absence of e-procurement systems in fully adopting sustainable procurement practices that integrate environmental criteria into resource acquisition decisions to reduce waste generation. (Martens and Schwarz 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Nature capital involves using financial tools and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and drive sustainable development. (HAVEMAN 1992) degree: 479 citations: 479
Nature Capital involves investing in and financing the protection and restoration of ecosystems, promoting biodiversity conservation and sustainable development through economic incentives and financial tools. (SB. Moore and Manring 2009) degree: 293 citations: 293
Circular innovations like salt recovery and tannin baths reuse in an Italian tannery demonstrated strong financial sustainability benefits through increased profitability and positive net present value over 10 years. (Dainelli, Daddi, and Marrucci 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2

Cluster 10-1—: Microfinance Institutions

Documents: 128; Ave. Year: 2019.4; Ave. Citations: 19.7
The cluster of texts investigates the complex interplay of factors that shape the sustainability and performance of microfinance institutions (MFIs). Researchers examine how capital structure, financing sources, governance mechanisms, intellectual capital, and the broader economic environment influence MFIs’ ability to achieve their dual objectives of financial viability and social impact. The studies highlight the importance of understanding the determinants of MFI success, such as the role of retained earnings, equity, and external governance in driving financial performance and outreach. Additionally, the texts explore how macroeconomic conditions, including economic growth and financial depth, as well as the overall economic landscape, such as manufacturing and workforce participation, affect the operations and outcomes of MFIs. By shedding light on these critical factors, the cluster contributes to the ongoing discourse on how to optimize the sustainability and performance of MFIs, ultimately supporting their mission of promoting financial inclusion and alleviating poverty.

Articles: Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and support sustainable development. (Hermes and Lensink 2007) degree: 229 citations: 229
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and achieve sustainable development. (Conning 1999) degree: 190 citations: 190
The study found a positive relationship between capital structure and the financial and social performance of Indian microfinance institutions, suggesting they can maintain dual objectives of outreach and sustainability after transformation. (Chauhan, Verma, and Kumar 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Nature Capital involves using financial tools and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and support sustainable development. (Wirasedana et al. 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
The study examines how different financing sources and external governance influence the self-sustainability of microfinance institutions working towards poverty reduction goals, using data from 661 MFIs across 86 countries from 2010-2018. (Ahamad et al. 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
The study examines the relationship between microfinance institution (MFI) performance and macroeconomic factors, finding evidence of complementarity and substitutability between MFIs and the broader economy in terms of cost coverage, default rates, and outreach. (Ahlin, Lin, and Maio 2011) degree: 244 citations: 244

Cluster 10-99—: Sustainable Business Models and Practices for Environmental and Social Impact

Documents: 545; Ave. Year: 2017.9; Ave. Citations: 21.7
The texts explore various approaches and frameworks for businesses to incorporate sustainability, environmental protection, and social impact into their operations and decision-making processes. Cluster 11-1—: Factors Influencing Farmers’ Adaptation to Climate Change and Barriers to Adaptation

Documents: 91; Ave. Year: 2019.6; Ave. Citations: 39.2
The texts collectively explore the complex interplay of factors that influence farmers’ adaptation strategies in response to climate change and the barriers they encounter in the process. Farmers employ various adaptation measures, such as modifying crop varieties, implementing soil conservation practices, adjusting planting schedules, and adopting irrigation techniques. Their adaptation choices are shaped by access to resources, including credit, extension services, climate information, land, and social capital. However, farmers also face significant barriers that hinder their ability to effectively adapt, such as limited access to information, financial constraints, and social barriers encompassing cognitive, normative, and institutional factors. The texts emphasize the crucial role of livelihood assets, including human, social, financial, natural, and physical capital, in either enabling or constraining farmers’ adaptive capacity and resilience in the face of climate change.

Cluster 13-0—: Resilience

Documents: 311; Ave. Year: 2019.8; Ave. Citations: 21.1
The texts in this cluster explore the concept of resilience across various domains, including social policy, urban planning, entrepreneurship, and financial systems. They highlight the importance of building resilience at different levels - individual, institutional, urban, and financial - to effectively cope with shocks, crises, and transitions. The articles discuss how resilience can be enhanced through capacity-building mechanisms, adaptive strategies, and supportive policies. For instance, the EU’s capacity-building mechanisms can help buffer national welfare states during economic shocks, while climate resilience frameworks can guide urban planning and adaptation to climate change impacts. The texts also examine the role of resilience in enabling entrepreneurs to rebound from failures and start new ventures, as well as the factors influencing the resilience of banking systems during economic transitions. Overall, the cluster emphasizes the significance of resilience in promoting socio-economic and environmental sustainability, particularly in the face of challenges such as the COVID-19 pandemic and climate change.

Cluster 2-10—: Valuation of Natural Capital and Ecosystem Services through Emergy Analysis

Documents: 118; Ave. Year: 2015.5; Ave. Citations: 38.3
The texts explore the application of emergy analysis, a method that evaluates the flows of energy and resources in ecosystems, to assess and value natural capital and the ecosystem services it provides. By using emergy-based indices, such as the emergy yield ratio, researchers can evaluate the sustainability of resource use and account for environmental impacts. This approach has been applied to value specific ecosystems, including seagrass meadows, marine ecosystems, and coralligenous habitats, emphasizing their importance as natural capital stocks that generate crucial ecosystem services. The texts highlight the need for proper valuation and monitoring of natural capital to support conservation efforts and sustainable management practices.

Articles: Using emergy-based indices and ratios, this paper evaluates the sustainable use of resources by analyzing energy and resource flows within ecosystems, and proposes modifying the emergy yield ratio to account for present and future environmental damages. (Ulgiati et al. 1995) degree: 184 citations: 184

Cluster 2-14—: Geodiversity and Natural Capital

Documents: 83; Ave. Year: 2017.9; Ave. Citations: 49
The texts collectively emphasize the importance of integrating geodiversity, which encompasses the non-living components of nature such as rocks, minerals, and landforms, into existing frameworks like natural capital and ecosystem services. These frameworks have traditionally focused on biodiversity and living components, but achieving sustainable development goals requires recognizing the crucial role played by abiotic elements in providing societal benefits and supporting ecosystems. By incorporating geodiversity and “geosystem services” alongside biodiversity and ecosystem services, a more holistic and integrated approach to managing and valuing nature’s contributions can be achieved, leading to more effective sustainable development policies and practices.

Cluster 2-2—: Natural Capital Accounting and Ecosystem Services

Cluster 2-6—: Natural Capital Accounting and Assessment

Documents: 188; Ave. Year: 2017.1; Ave. Citations: 48.6
The texts explore various methods and frameworks for accounting for and assessing the use of natural capital - the stock of renewable and non-renewable natural resources that provide vital ecosystem services. One key approach is the ecological footprint, which quantifies human demand on natural capital and compares it against the regenerative biocapacity of the biosphere. By applying these natural capital accounting techniques to specific regions over time, researchers aim to analyze the supply, demand, flows and deficits of natural resources in order to promote sustainable development by maintaining adequate natural capital stocks. However, economic rationality and market failures present barriers to investing in the replenishment of natural capital from this biophysical accounting perspective.

Articles: The study develops an accounting framework for ecological compensation standards in Xinjiang, China, based on natural capital supply and demand, integrating ecological footprint, natural capital monetization, and socioeconomic factors, revealing an increasing ecological deficit and compensation needs from 2010 to 2020. (Shi et al. 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
Utilizing a regional-industrial perspective and a localized three-dimensional ecological footprint model, this study measured and decomposed natural capital use in Xinjiang from 2005 to 2020, revealing the region’s ecological deficit, resource allocation imbalances, industrial structural inefficiencies, and driving factors behind ecological pressures. (Jin et al. 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
Using the ecological footprint concept, this study presents a framework to quantify a nation’s consumption of natural capital by measuring its energy and resource use in terms of the biologically productive area required to sustain it. (Wackernagel et al. 1999) degree: 1385 citations: 1385

Cluster 2-7—: Ecological Restoration and Biodiversity Offsets

Documents: 145; Ave. Year: 2015; Ave. Citations: 36.1
The cluster of texts critically examines the effectiveness and limitations of using ecological restoration as a strategy for biodiversity conservation and offsetting losses from development projects. While restoration projects aim to provide societal and biodiversity benefits, studies find that the links between restoration and these outcomes are often weakly demonstrated. Even wildlife-friendly practices in plantations fail to adequately protect biodiversity compared to intact forests. Biodiversity offset policies place high expectations on restoration to compensate for losses, but evidence is limited that restoration can reliably achieve “no net loss” due to factors like time lags and uncertainty in measuring biodiversity values. The texts call for stronger integration of restoration science into offset policies, and for these policies to better account for restoration’s limitations. Additionally, current tools for businesses to measure their biodiversity impacts have major gaps in aligning with recent global frameworks for corporate reporting on nature impacts. Overall, the cluster highlights the need for a critical examination of the role and limitations of ecological restoration within biodiversity conservation policies and corporate environmental accountability.

Articles: In peer-reviewed publications from 2000-2008, restoration practitioners inadequately quantified the socioeconomic benefits of ecological restoration projects that could justify them as worthwhile investments for society. (Aronson et al. 2010) degree: 216 citations: 216
Retaining forest fragments within oil palm plantations fails to effectively protect bird biodiversity, with imperiled species far less abundant in fragments and plantations compared to contiguous forests. (Edwards et al. 2010) degree: 204 citations: 204
Nature Capital involves co-crediting carbon sequestration and biodiversity conservation through molecular analysis of soil biodiversity and remote sensing of aboveground ecosystems to incentivize industry towards environmentally sustainable land management. (Tedersoo et al. 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Ecological restoration as a means to offset biodiversity loss through development projects faces significant challenges due to time lags, uncertainty, and measurability issues, limiting its ability to reliably achieve “no net loss” goals currently set by offset policies. (Maron et al. 2012) degree: 420 citations: 420
Nature Capital initiatives need to prioritize social justice by addressing existing power imbalances and inequities that could be amplified by large international funding for nature conservation and restoration efforts. (Milner-Gulland 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Existing biodiversity indices have glaring gaps in covering the metrics required by the Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Framework for businesses to measure and report their impacts on nature. (Zhu et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2

Cluster 3-6—: Digital Finance and Green Technology Innovation

Documents: 195; Ave. Year: 2022.8; Ave. Citations: 17.3
The cluster of texts highlights the transformative potential of digital finance in catalyzing green technological innovation and fostering environmental sustainability. By harnessing advanced technologies like big data, digital finance alleviates financing constraints faced by enterprises engaging in green innovation activities. This enhanced access to capital positively impacts both the quantity and quality of green technological innovation across cities and regions. Moreover, digital finance emerges as a key driver of green economic growth and urban sustainable development by stimulating green tech innovation, enhancing human capital, and improving energy-environmental performance through increased efficiency. The positive effects of digital finance on green tech innovation are further reinforced by environmental decentralization and regulations. However, the impact exhibits heterogeneity, with regions characterized by immature financial markets and smaller firms reaping greater benefits. Overall, digital finance serves as a powerful tool for mobilizing investments and nurturing innovations that safeguard the environment, conserve natural resources, and align economic development with sustainable green objectives.

Articles: Digital finance alleviates financing constraints, thereby promoting the quantity and quality of green technological innovation in Chinese cities. (BQ. Lin and Ma 2022) degree: 157 citations: 157

(Y. Liu, Xie, and Zhong 2024) degree: 12 citations: 12
Digital finance exhibits a spatial threshold effect on green economic growth, with higher levels promoting local growth but negatively spilling over to surrounding areas, though this effect attenuates with distance. (Pang, Liu, and Hua 2024) degree: 7 citations: 7

Cluster 4-1—: Nature Capital

Documents: 336; Ave. Year: 2016.8; Ave. Citations: 52.7
The texts collectively explore the growing trend of commodifying and economically valuing nature and natural resources under the framework of “Nature Capital”. This approach involves the use of market-based mechanisms, financial instruments, and novel forms of valuation to assign economic value to various aspects of the environment, such as carbon sequestration, biodiversity conservation, and ecosystem services. While presented as a solution to the ecological crisis, this commodification of nature has been critiqued for reinforcing capitalist representations of the environment and facilitating the appropriation and alienation of land and resources for environmental ends, often driven by the interests of new actors like venture capitalists and financial institutions. The texts highlight the challenges and contradictions that arise when conservation goals are aligned with financial motives, as well as the broader implications of this neoliberal restructuring of environmental governance for control, exploitation, and the distribution of resources. Ultimately, the commodification of nature under the “Nature Capital” framework raises important questions about the effectiveness and equity of market-oriented approaches to addressing environmental challenges.

Articles: Nature Capital proponents claim valuing nature economically makes the ecological crisis tangible, yet this valuation-centric approach has rarely been implemented and lacks evidence of conservation outcomes. (Maechler and Boisvert 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
Using advanced measurement technologies, private companies in Scotland can identify and verify more carbon credits from forests without increasing actual carbon sequestration, generating financial value through a shift in knowledge practices rather than material changes. (Stanley 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
The Rhino Bond, the first financial instrument dedicated to protecting a species, allows investors to invest in black rhinoceros conservation, with returns tied to population increases, favoring large, easily monitored species and financially sustainable sites, highlighting conservation finance’s limitations. (Medina and Scales 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Neoliberal restructuring projects are path-dependent, contextually embedded processes that creatively destroy political-economic space at multiple scales, with cities playing a crucial strategic role. (Brenner and Theodore 2002) degree: 2412 citations: 2412
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity conservation, and support sustainable development. (Ritzer and Jurgenson 2010) degree: 1415 citations: 1415
Green grabbing refers to the appropriation of land and resources under the guise of environmental protection, leading to alienation of local communities from their traditional lands and resources. (Fairhead, Leach, and Scoones 2012) degree: 658 citations: 658

Cluster 4-10—: Social Sciences and Climate Change

Documents: 88; Ave. Year: 2020.3; Ave. Citations: 21.3
The texts collectively emphasize the critical yet underappreciated role of social sciences in addressing the complex challenges of climate change adaptation and mitigation. Despite the crucial insights that social sciences provide into the political, economic, and societal factors influencing climate change responses, research and funding have disproportionately focused on the natural and technical sciences. This imbalance has led to a gap in understanding the human dimensions of climate change, such as the development of effective adaptation policies, climate finance mechanisms, and social protection systems. To bridge this gap, the texts call for increased investment in social science research, capacity building, and interdisciplinary collaboration. By strengthening the social science component of climate change research and action, we can develop more holistic and equitable approaches to adaptation and mitigation, particularly for vulnerable populations.

Articles: Focusing on Nature Capital, the text highlights the significant underfunding of social science research related to climate change mitigation, with only 0.12% of research funding allocated to understanding the social and economic aspects of transitioning to a sustainable economy. (Overland and Sovacool 2020) degree: 137 citations: 137
Political scientists largely overlook the critical field of climate change adaptation, which is fundamentally political in nature and requires expertise in areas like political economy, justice, comparative politics, and various levels of government. (Javeline 2014) degree: 111 citations: 111
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives, financial tools, and investments to protect and restore ecosystems, promote biodiversity, and support sustainable development. (Sorensen and Fried 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Channeling loss and damage funding through national social protection systems in climate-vulnerable countries could efficiently contribute to safeguarding development gains, addressing non-economic losses and damages, and fostering long-term climate resilience while aligning with human rights, climate justice, and equity principles. (Huber and Murray 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
The study analyzes how the Green Climate Fund, the largest multilateral fund under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change, can finance social protection initiatives to promote low-carbon, climate-resilient development in low- and middle-income countries through its project portfolio focused on Nature Capital. (Aleksandrova, Kuhl, and Malerba 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
Reconceptualizing adaptation to climate change as dynamic pathways that integrate incremental actions addressing proximate causes with transformative societal changes to overcome constraints imposed by vested interests, institutions, and path dependencies. (Wise et al. 2014) degree: 674 citations: 674

Cluster 4-2—: Climate Change Vulnerability and Adaptation for Developing Countries

Documents: 324; Ave. Year: 2018.9; Ave. Citations: 23.5
The texts collectively examine the complex landscape of climate change vulnerability and adaptation in developing countries. They highlight the importance of accurately measuring and representing the disproportionate impacts faced by these nations, which often contribute the least to global emissions yet bear the brunt of the consequences. This inequity underscores the urgent need for financial support and technology transfer from developed countries to enable sustainable adaptation measures. Effective adaptation requires context-specific approaches that leverage local knowledge and are guided by overarching principles of equity and resilience. International cooperation, agreements, and funding mechanisms play a crucial role in facilitating this process. Ultimately, addressing the adaptation needs of developing countries is framed as a matter of climate justice and a shared global responsibility.

Poor nations that have contributed little to climate change face higher vulnerability in terms of food security, human health, and coastal populations due to their lower adaptive capacity, biophysical sensitivity, and socio-economic exposure compared to wealthier nations. Cluster 4-4—: Finance, Investment, and Inequality

The texts in this cluster explore the complex interplay between finance, investment, and various forms of inequality across different contexts. They highlight how financial flows, investment decisions, and financing mechanisms can either exacerbate or alleviate inequalities, particularly in relation to climate change, sustainable development, and environmental justice. Together, these studies underscore the critical importance of understanding and addressing the nexus between finance, investment, and inequality in the pursuit of a more sustainable and equitable future.

Cluster 4-6—: Innovative Financing for Marine Conservation

Documents: 146; Ave. Year: 2018.7; Ave. Citations: 22.5
The texts in this cluster explore various innovative financing mechanisms and economic instruments that can be leveraged to generate sustainable funds for environmental conservation and management, with a particular focus on marine and coastal ecosystems such as mangroves, seagrasses, and coral reefs. These approaches include contingent valuation methods to assess willingness to pay (WTP) and implement user fees in marine protected areas (MPAs), debt-for-nature swaps to restructure debt obligations and channel funds towards conservation, blue carbon accounting to monetize the carbon sequestration potential of coastal habitats, and blue bonds to attract impact investments in ocean health projects. By utilizing market-based tools, economic incentives, and innovative investment strategies, these financing mechanisms aim to promote sustainable management of valuable natural capital and biodiversity in marine and coastal environments, contributing to the broader context of green finance and sustainable development.

Articles: Foreign visitors to Nepal’s Annapurna Conservation Area expressed a high willingness to pay increased entry fees to better protect the environment and promote sustainable local development, with a median willingness to pay of $74.3 USD. (Baral, Stern, and Bhattarai 2008) degree: 180 citations: 180
The text highlights strategies to address critical challenges faced by the ocean, including integrating sciences, improving ocean-observing systems, enhancing science-policy interfaces, establishing new partnerships and finance systems, and promoting ocean literacy, to support sustainable development and transformative action. (Claudet et al. 2020) degree: 152 citations: 152
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and financial tools to protect ecosystems, promote biodiversity, and fund sustainable management of marine protected areas, as demonstrated by divers’ willingness to pay higher user fees for access to the Bonaire National Marine Park without significantly impacting tourism. (Thur 2010) degree: 145 citations: 145
Debt-for-nature swaps could protect up to 100% of unprotected biodiversity priority areas in 35 out of 67 countries at risk of sovereign debt distress by exchanging a fraction of their debt. (Nedopil, Yue, and Hughes 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
This paper presents China’s first national-scale systematic Blue Carbon accounting system for mangroves, salt marshes, and seagrasses, analyzing their restoration potential and application for achieving China’s carbon neutrality goals under the Paris Agreement. (JW. Liu, Failler, and Ramrattan 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
The Seychelles’ blue bond, a financial innovation to finance sustainable blue economy activities, prioritizes commercial interests over local community needs and alternative fisheries proposals, leading to ecological restructuring and indebtedness for climate adaptation due to limited financing options. (Kiliç 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1

Cluster 4-8—: Climate Change Impacts and Adaptation Strategies

Documents: 98; Ave. Year: 2017; Ave. Citations: 47.2
The texts collectively underscore the far-reaching impacts of climate change on various sectors, including agriculture, food security, economic development, and human habitats. They highlight the vulnerability of these sectors to climate-related factors such as rising temperatures, rainfall variability, and extreme weather events. To address these challenges, the texts explore a range of adaptation strategies and mitigation measures, including the adoption of sustainable practices, implementation of policy interventions, and development of climate-smart approaches. These strategies aim to enhance resilience, promote sustainable development, and protect valuable natural resources, ultimately contributing to the long-term sustainability of ecosystems and human societies in the face of a changing climate.

Cluster 5-2—: Central Banking and Climate Finance

Documents: 270; Ave. Year: 2021.7; Ave. Citations: 16.2
The texts collectively highlight the growing recognition of climate change as a significant risk to financial stability and the potential role of central banks and financial regulators in addressing this challenge. Climate change can impact asset prices, firm profitability, and loan quality, thereby posing risks to the stability of the financial system. In response, central banks and regulators are exploring various measures to mitigate these climate-related financial risks and facilitate the transition to a low-carbon economy. These measures include climate stress testing to assess the resilience of financial institutions, green quantitative easing to support low-carbon investments, differentiated reserve requirements to incentivize green lending, and other monetary and macroprudential policies. The texts emphasize the need for comprehensive analytical frameworks to assess climate risks within the financial system and the importance of aligning financial flows with the goals of the Paris Agreement. As the attention to this issue grows, central banks and financial regulators are increasingly recognizing their role in promoting a sustainable and resilient financial system in the face of climate change.

Cluster 6-2—: FDI and Environmental Sustainability

Documents: 166; Ave. Year: 2020.3; Ave. Citations: 34
The texts collectively investigate the complex relationship between foreign direct investment (FDI) and environmental sustainability across various regions and income groups. While some studies suggest that FDI may lead to increased environmental degradation, supporting the pollution haven hypothesis, others find evidence for the pollution halo hypothesis, where FDI promotes green growth and efficiency. The impact of FDI on carbon dioxide emissions, urban green development, and overall environmental quality is examined, with attention to the moderating roles of factors such as trade, urbanization, intellectual property rights, and environmental regulations. The common theme emerging from these texts is the need to understand the nuanced effects of FDI on environmental sustainability and to develop policies that encourage green growth and mitigate potential negative consequences.

Cluster 6-4—: Natural Resource Curse

Documents: 134; Ave. Year: 2020.5; Ave. Citations: 32.6
The relationship between natural resource abundance and economic growth has been a subject of much research and debate, with many studies finding evidence of a “resource curse” where resource-rich countries paradoxically tend to have slower growth than those without such endowments. Potential explanations for this phenomenon include Dutch disease effects that crowd out other sectors, rent-seeking behavior by elites, volatility of resource revenues, and lack of incentives to develop other industries. However, the resource curse is not inevitable, and some countries have successfully harnessed their natural wealth for development through sound policies such as good governance, productive investment of resource revenues, and a focus on building human capital and economic complexity. Factors such as the quality of institutions, financial development, industrialization, trade openness, and corruption can all mediate the impact of natural resources on growth. Ultimately, the key to overcoming the resource curse and achieving sustainable development lies in implementing a comprehensive policy framework to manage resource wealth effectively.

Articles: Human capital development helps mitigate the resource curse by enabling more efficient utilization of natural resources, promoting economic growth in Next Eleven countries. (Rahim et al. 2021) degree: 183 citations: 183
Natural resource extraction, particularly mining, presents both opportunities and challenges for economic development in resource-rich countries, necessitating tailored policies to maximize its positive impacts while mitigating potential negative consequences. (Davis and Tilton 2005) degree: 196 citations: 196
Financial globalization, green innovation, and efficient financial risk management can significantly reduce energy-related greenhouse gas emissions in G7 countries, while economic growth and human capital increase emissions. (YH. Chen et al. 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3
This study investigates the complex interplay between natural resources, economic complexity, and sustainable economic growth within G-20 countries from 1990 to 2021, finding that while natural resources, economic complexity, corruption, and excessive capital formation hinder growth, foreign investment, trade openness, and labor force promote it. (C. Zhang et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
Nigeria’s governance of oil revenues institutionalized rent-seeking practices by political elites, perpetuating the resource curse and underdevelopment, but establishing clear fiscal rules and investment in natural capital could mitigate this. (Asiegbu et al. 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
Natural capital, which includes natural resources, has been linked to slower economic growth and development across countries, potentially due to factors such as Dutch disease and rent-seeking behavior. (Gylfason 2001) degree: 1048 citations: 1048

Cluster 6-5—: Economic Factors and Environmental Sustainability Across Regions

They employ different methodologies, such as panel data analysis, spatial models, and surveys, to investigate the causal links and effects of these economic factors on environmental degradation or conservation efforts. The common topic shared by these texts can be summarized as “Economic Factors and Environmental Sustainability Across Regions.”

Articles: The study examines the impact of trade balance, economic growth, green investment, energy use, financial development, and urbanization on environmental sustainability in BRICS countries from 1991-2020, finding that increasing the export-to-import ratio improves environmental conditions through Nature Capital mechanisms. Cluster 6-8—: Environmental Sustainability Drivers

Documents: 75; Ave. Year: 2021.4; Ave. Citations: 32.9
The texts in this cluster explore the complex interplay between economic activities, environmental policies, and technological innovations in shaping the path towards environmental sustainability. While economic activities such as mineral extraction, industrialization, urbanization, and foreign direct investment have been linked to natural resource depletion, increased ecological footprint, and environmental degradation, particularly in terms of carbon emissions, the adoption of renewable energy, green technologies, and sustainable finance has emerged as a promising solution to mitigate these negative impacts. Moreover, eco-digitalization and environmental diplomacy have been highlighted as key enablers of sustainable development. The studies in this cluster employ econometric models and panel data analysis to investigate the relationship between these factors and environmental indicators across different country groups. The findings suggest that a comprehensive approach encompassing economic, policy, and technological dimensions is crucial for reducing emissions, minimizing ecological footprint, and achieving long-term environmental sustainability.

Cluster 6-99—: Sustainable Economic Development and Environmental Impacts

Documents: 310; Ave. Year: 2020.6; Ave. Citations: 25.2
The cluster of texts explores the complex interplay between economic growth, energy consumption, environmental innovations, and foreign investment, and their collective impact on carbon emissions, ecological footprint, and the attainment of sustainable development goals. The overarching theme that emerges from these texts is the need to strike a balance between economic progress and environmental protection, identifying pathways for sustainable development through the adoption of renewable energy, green innovations, and responsible management of natural resources, all of which are essential components of leveraging Nature Capital for long-term sustainability and growth.

Cluster 7-1—: Green Bonds

Documents: 489; Ave. Year: 2022; Ave. Citations: 16.7
The growing market for green bonds, a type of debt instrument designed to finance environmentally-friendly projects and initiatives, is the focus of these texts. They analyze various factors influencing green bond issuance, such as national culture, industry characteristics, and investor preferences, while also assessing the impact of green bonds on bank efficiency, stock prices, institutional ownership, and environmental performance. Some texts also discuss sustainability-linked bonds, which tie general debt financing to sustainability targets. Collectively, these articles examine the potential of green bonds to promote sustainable development, reduce emissions, and support the transition to a low-carbon economy, highlighting their role and impact in the realm of sustainable finance.

Articles: Green credit has a negative impact on bank efficiency, but the effect varies among bank types, with smaller and less capitalized banks being more affected, while highly capitalized and large banks involved in green credit benefit from this activity. (Gal’an and Tan 2024) degree: 10 citations: 10
Countries’ cultural values, such as individualism, masculinity, and indulgence, negatively impact green bond issuance, while long-term orientation and uncertainty avoidance positively influence it, suggesting culture plays a role in the development of green bond markets. (Mertzanis and Tebourbi 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
Sustainability-linked bonds tie general debt financing to issuers’ sustainability targets, but their potential to drive climate transitions hinges on common standards, science-based targets, avoiding greenwashing concerns, and meaningful incentives for improved carbon performance. (Vulturius, Maltais, and Forsbacka 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5
Corporate green bonds finance climate-friendly projects, signaling issuers’ environmental commitment through third-party certification, leading to positive investor response, improved environmental performance, and increased ownership by long-term and green investors. (Flammer 2021) degree: 460 citations: 460
Green bonds exhibit a small negative yield premium of around 2 basis points compared to conventional bonds, suggesting pro-environmental investor preferences have a limited impact on bond prices. (Zerbib 2019) degree: 441 citations: 441
Green bond issuance by firms positively impacts stock prices, increases domestic institutional ownership and improves stock liquidity, benefiting existing shareholders, despite no consistent evidence of a premium for green bonds. (Tang and Zhang 2020) degree: 346 citations: 346

Cluster 7-2—: Connectedness between Green Energy Investments and Conventional Financial Markets

Documents: 326; Ave. Year: 2022.5; Ave. Citations: 20
The texts collectively explore the intricate web of connectedness between green energy investments, such as green bonds and clean energy stocks, and conventional financial markets, including crude oil, energy ETFs, and broader stock markets. This interconnectedness manifests through various channels, including return and volatility spillovers, co-movement patterns, and the transmission of shocks across these seemingly distinct asset classes. Researchers employ diverse methodologies, such as quantile-based approaches, structural VAR models, and analyses of downside risk, to uncover the nuances of these relationships. Furthermore, the studies highlight the influence of exogenous factors, such as geopolitical risks, economic policy uncertainty, and the COVID-19 pandemic, on the dynamics between green and conventional energy investments. Some texts also emphasize the potential role of emerging technologies, like artificial intelligence and fintech, in promoting eco-friendly investments and combating greenwashing. Ultimately, understanding these complex interconnections is crucial for investors and policymakers seeking to navigate the evolving landscape of energy investments, manage risks, and foster a sustainable financial system.

Articles: Nature Capital investments exhibit extreme connectedness with dirty energy assets like crude oil and energy ETFs, reaching up to 65% during extreme market conditions, compared to an average of 29%, highlighting the need for quantile-based measures to capture tail risks. (Saeed, Bouri, and Alsulami 2021) degree: 213 citations: 213
The study examines the downside risk connectedness between Islamic sectors and green bond markets, developing a framework to construct hedging and portfolio strategies, revealing time-varying connectedness influenced by major events, with implications for investment and risk management. (Billah et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
The study examines how geopolitical risk and economic policy uncertainty influence the dynamic connectedness between clean energy, conventional energy, and food markets during major crises and events. (Yousfi and Bouzgarrou 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
Green bonds exhibit strong co-movement with corporate and treasury bond markets, limited diversification benefits for fixed-income investors, but offer diversification for stock and energy investors, with prices influenced by corporate and treasury bond markets. (Reboredo 2018) degree: 332 citations: 332
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and financial tools to protect ecosystems, with the green bond market closely linked to fixed-income and currency markets, receiving sizeable spillovers while transmitting negligible reverse effects. (Reboredo and Ugolini 2020) degree: 231 citations: 231
This study investigates the roles of fintech and AI in promoting eco-friendly investments, combating greenwashing, and fostering sustainable finance in the US market. (Mohammed et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2

Cluster 7-3—: The Nexus between Environmental Factors and Green Finance Market Dynamics

The studies provide valuable insights for stakeholders such as investors, policymakers, and portfolio managers, shedding light on the ability of green bonds to genuinely represent environmental values while navigating the complex landscape shaped by the ever-evolving climate and environmental factors.

Cluster 7-99—: ESG and Investor Sentiment in Sustainable Finance

Cluster 8-2—: Impact of Green Finance Policies on Firms in China

Cluster 8-3—: Green Credit Financing

Documents: 145; Ave. Year: 2022; Ave. Citations: 18.4
The texts in this cluster explore the impact of green credit financing (GCF) on corporate environmental sustainability. GCF is a financial mechanism where banks provide loans or credit to companies contingent on their commitment to environmentally sustainable practices, such as reducing carbon emissions, investing in green technologies, and promoting renewable energy. The studies examine the effectiveness of GCF in comparison to traditional financing methods, the role of government policies and regulations, and the economic and environmental implications of GCF adoption. They analyze how GCF influences supply chain dynamics, corporate green innovation, emission reduction investments, and overall green economy development, ultimately assessing its potential to drive positive change in corporate environmental sustainability.

Articles: Nature Capital financing encourages sustainable investment through green loans requiring environmental compliance, enabling win-win situations for supply chains under strict carbon emission policies. (SM. An et al. 2021) degree: 221 citations: 221
The study finds that China’s Green Credit Guidelines significantly improved the quality of green innovation in heavily polluting enterprises, with stronger effects in state-owned firms, eastern regions, and less financially developed areas. (HT. Wang et al. 2022) degree: 144 citations: 144
Nature Capital investments aim to protect ecosystems and biodiversity through economic incentives and financial tools, promoting sustainable development. (Dou and Choi 2024) degree: 4 citations: 4
Green credit regulation decreases green investment efficiency for polluting firms, who prioritize symbolic efforts over substantive green investments, but this effect can be mitigated by stronger environmental law enforcement, financial development, and intellectual property protection. (JF. Tian et al. 2024) degree: 2 citations: 2
The study investigates the non-linear relationship between renewable energy investment, green credit, and green economy development for 150 Chinese listed companies, finding dual threshold effects where the impact transitions from promoting to restraining and then promoting again. (LY. He et al. 2019) degree: 297 citations: 297
Manufacturers’ emission-reduction investments in competing supply chains exhibit a Prisoner’s Dilemma scenario at low carbon tax rates, where abandoning investments cracks the dilemma but shrinks economic-environmental coexistence. (C. Zhang, Xiao, and Xu 2024) degree: 5 citations: 5

The texts collectively evaluate the implementation and impacts of China’s green credit policy, which aims to promote environmental protection by regulating credit to companies based on their environmental performance. Cluster 8-5—: Green Credit Policies and Sustainable Innovation in China

Documents: 101; Ave. Year: 2022.4; Ave. Citations: 14
These texts examine the role of green finance policies, specifically green credit policies implemented in China, in promoting environmentally sustainable practices and technologies among enterprises and industries. The studies analyze the impact of these policies on various aspects, including green technology innovation, carbon emission reduction in heavily polluting industries, low-carbon technology adoption, digital innovation for sustainability, and overall green transformation of enterprises. The findings suggest that green credit policies have generally been effective in driving green technology innovation, reducing carbon emission intensity, and promoting low-carbon technologies, particularly in state-owned and larger enterprises. However, the impact on digital innovation appears to be negative. The studies also explore the mechanisms through which these policies operate, such as financing constraints, R&D investment, and management efficiency.

Articles: Green credit guidelines issued by China’s banking regulator in 2012 promoted green technology innovation among state-owned and large enterprises by limiting debt financing for environmentally harmful activities. (Hong, Li, and Drakeford 2021) degree: 118 citations: 118
The green credit policy in China, treated as a quasi-natural experiment, reduced the carbon emission intensity of heavily polluting industries by 0.267 tons per 10,000 yuan per year compared to non-polluting industries through resource allocation and green innovation effects. (AX. Zhang, Deng, and Wu 2022) degree: 95 citations: 95
Green credit policy significantly promotes low-carbon technology innovation in Chinese enterprises, especially state-owned and ESG-certified firms, by increasing their R&D investment and management efficiency. (ZG. Chen et al. 2022) degree: 90 citations: 90
Green credit policies inhibit digital innovation in heavily-polluting Chinese listed companies by increasing financing constraints and crowding out R&D investment, with the negative impact being more pronounced in state-owned enterprises, firms without financially experienced executives, and those in eastern regions. (Q. Lu et al. 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1
This paper investigates whether Chinese government guidance funds promote green transformation in enterprises, finding that such funds significantly improve enterprises’ green transformation, especially for state-owned, heavily polluting, and financially constrained enterprises. (JH. Sun et al. 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
Digital technology application significantly increases firms’ green product export by reducing costs, enhancing technology, and expanding markets, especially for non-polluting, state-owned, and high environmental investment firms in regions with strict regulations, developed digital infrastructure, and strong IP protection. Cluster 8-6—: Positive Resources for Environmental Sustainability

Documents: 73; Ave. Year: 2018.8; Ave. Citations: 35.9
The texts in this cluster explore various positive resources and factors that can be leveraged to promote environmental sustainability and address pressing environmental challenges. These include the potential of organizational support and psychological capital in enhancing work performance and effectiveness, which could indirectly contribute to sustainability efforts. The role of green human capital and innovation in driving sustainable economic growth is also highlighted, although this may come with the risk of increasing inequality between skills. The interaction between market failures, environmental pollution, and technological innovation is another key theme, emphasizing the need for policies that encourage emissions reduction and the development of eco-friendly technologies. The recognition heuristic is presented as an adaptive strategy for making inferences from missing knowledge patterns, which could be applied in environmental decision-making contexts. The Blue Economy framework is also discussed as a critical approach for preserving natural capital and aligning economic growth with environmental conservation goals. Finally, the positive influence of government environmental attention on firms’ green investment is explored, with green credit supply and environmental regulations acting as mediating factors. Together, these texts underscore the importance of identifying and mobilizing positive resources, strategies, and frameworks to advance environmental sustainability and protect valuable natural capital.

Articles: Perceived organizational support and psychological capital, especially resilience and optimism, are positive resources that can help combat depressive symptoms among Chinese male correctional officers, partially mediating the association between organizational support and depression. (L. Liu et al. 2013) degree: 92 citations: 92
The paper explores how green innovation and green human capital may contribute to higher inequality within and between skill groups, proposing a theoretical framework and empirical observations from over 2,000 companies across 21 OECD countries in 2022, with policy implications discussed in light of COVID-19 recovery plans emphasizing green investment. (Crifo 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0
Nature Capital involves using economic incentives and investments to address combined market failures related to environmental pollution and innovation diffusion, fostering emissions reduction and environmentally beneficial technologies. (Jaffe, Newell, and Stavins 2005) degree: 921 citations: 921
In the context of Nature Capital, the recognition heuristic exploits organisms’ ability to recognize patterns of missing knowledge, leading to accurate inferences when less knowledge is better than more. (Goldstein and Gigerenzer 2002) degree: 929 citations: 929
The Blue Economy plays a critical role in preserving natural capital and aligning economic growth with environmental conservation for sustainable development. (Ovchynnykova, Martinkiene, and Navickas 2024) degree: 0 citations: 0

Cluster 8-7—: Green Finance and Environmental Risk Management in Banking

Documents: 71; Ave. Year: 2019.5; Ave. Citations: 23.5
The texts collectively highlight the growing significance of green finance and environmental risk management in the banking sector. They explore various green finance policies and initiatives, such as those implemented in China, which aim to promote environmental protection, sustainable development, and address climate change risks. These policies encourage banks to integrate environmental risk assessments into their credit management processes, potentially influencing corporate credit ratings and debt costs. The texts also discuss the role of green innovation in improving corporate credit performance and the heterogeneous effects of green finance across different types of firms and regions, emphasizing the increasing importance of incorporating environmental considerations into the financial sector.

Articles: Nature Capital policies in China aim to use financial incentives and banking regulations to promote environmental protection and sustainable development. (Aizawa and Yang 2010) degree: 156 citations: 156
Canadian banks systematically examine environmental risks for credits, loans, and mortgages, integrating sustainability into their business strategies, making them proactive leaders in environmental risk management compared to global peers. (Weber 2012) degree: 129 citations: 129

Cluster 8-99—: Economic and Regulatory Drivers of Environmental Sustainability

Documents: 154; Ave. Year: 2020; Ave. Citations: 20.1
The texts in this cluster explore the complex interplay between economic factors, regulations, and environmental sustainability, focusing on how market forces, competition, deregulation, mergers and acquisitions, and entrepreneurial experimentation influence firms’ decisions to adopt cleaner practices, invest in environmental compliance, and innovate in areas such as low-carbon technologies. The studies highlight the role of government regulations, environmental policies, and economic incentives in shaping firms’ behavior towards more sustainable outcomes, such as emissions reduction and energy efficiency improvements. The common thread running through these texts is the examination of how various economic and regulatory drivers can either facilitate or hinder progress towards environmental sustainability goals in different contexts, from emissions trading programs and private firms’ polluting behavior to sustainable power supply investments and low-carbon mergers and acquisitions.

Articles: In a study on emissions trading and electricity restructuring, it was found that deregulated plants were less likely to adopt capital-intensive environmental compliance options, leading to higher emissions in states with more severe air quality issues. Cluster 9-1—: Green Intellectual Capital

Documents: 160; Ave. Year: 2021.4; Ave. Citations: 21.1
The texts explore the concept of “green intellectual capital” and its role in promoting sustainability and competitive advantage for firms. Green intellectual capital encompasses the knowledge, skills, systems, and relationships that enable companies to adopt environmentally sustainable practices. The research investigates how different components of green intellectual capital, including human, structural, and relational capital, impact various factors such as green human resource management, green technology innovation, and sustainable economic, environmental, and social performance. The findings suggest that nurturing green intellectual capital can help firms meet environmental regulations, enhance sustainability, and gain a competitive edge in their respective markets.

Articles: Green intellectual capital, comprising green human, relational and structural capital, influences green human resource management practices, according to a study of 112 Malaysian manufacturing firms. (Yong et al. 2019) degree: 225 citations: 225

(Yusliza et al. 2020) degree: 148 citations: 148
Green knowledge management enhances green technological innovation and sustainable performance in construction firms by leveraging green intellectual capital components, moderated by artificial intelligence’s impact on green human capital. (AN. Khan, Mehmood, and Kwan 2024) degree: 3 citations: 3

Cluster 9-2—: Green Innovation and Corporate Sustainability

Documents: 107; Ave. Year: 2021; Ave. Citations: 34.3
The texts collectively explore the concept of green or eco-innovation and its impact on corporate performance and the environment. Green innovation refers to the development and implementation of new products, processes, or business models that aim to reduce environmental impact and promote sustainability. The studies examine the drivers and impacts of green innovation on various aspects, including corporate financial performance, environmental performance (such as carbon emissions reduction), and the utilization of resources and capabilities. The texts highlight the positive relationship between green innovation and financial performance, suggesting that companies can benefit financially by adopting sustainable practices. Additionally, the role of financial tools like ESG funds and the impact of leverage on green innovation are discussed, emphasizing the importance of green innovation for achieving both economic and environmental sustainability goals.

Articles: Examining Finnish horse industry companies, this study found that valuing economic, institutional, and social sustainability drove investment and exploitation of green innovation, while environmental sustainability did not have an impact. (Saunila, Ukko, and Rantala 2018) degree: 205 citations: 205
Small and medium-sized enterprises can create responsible environmental and social innovations with different resource combinations, often relying on equity, research cooperation, networks, industry knowledge, and reputation, but technological innovations require more abundant resources. (Halme and Korpela 2014) degree: 191 citations: 191
Green R&D investment for eco-innovation reduces carbon emissions while improving financial performance of firms by leveraging unique resources and capabilities. (KH. Lee and Min 2015) degree: 587 citations: 587
Nature Capital involves investing in or financing the protection and restoration of the planet’s natural ecosystems, biodiversity, and sustainable development through economic incentives and financial tools. (Khalil, Khalil, and Khalil 2024) degree: 25 citations: 25
This study investigates the causal relationship between ESG funds, an environmentally friendly investment tool, and CO2 emissions as an environmental degradation indicator, exploring ESG funds’ potential as a strategic financial instrument for improving environmental quality during the COVID-19 period. (Tuna et al. 2024) degree: 4 citations: 4
Green innovation has a U-shaped relationship with corporate financial performance, and leverage ratio negatively impacts this relationship due to risk accumulation, while deleveraging measures enhance performance for firms investing in nature capital. (MY. Ai, Luo, and Bu 2024) degree: 1 citations: 1

By focusing on these key areas, organizations can effectively improve their environmental performance and play a crucial role in promoting sustainable development.

Cluster 9-4—: Sustainable Competitiveness and its Determinants

Documents: 77; Ave. Year: 2020.1; Ave. Citations: 18
The given cluster of texts collectively explores the concept of sustainable competitiveness and its various determinants. The studies investigate how factors such as firms’ strategic positioning, sustainability capital, intellectual capital, natural capital, social capital, economic sustainability, and governance contribute to achieving sustainable development goals and creating long-term value. The texts examine the relationships between these determinants and their impact on financial performance, risk management, and overall prosperity at both the company and country levels. By analyzing the relative importance of these pillars and their interactions, the studies aim to provide insights into the complex nature of sustainable competitiveness and its role in driving sustainable business models and economic growth.

Articles: Pursuing a differentiation strategy leads to more sustainable financial performance but higher risk compared to a cost leadership strategy, according to research analyzing 12,849 firm-year observations from 1989-2003. (Banker, Mashruwala, and Tripathy 2014) degree: 165 citations: 165

Cluster 9-99—: Sustainable Supply Chain Management and Circular Economy Practices

Documents: 396; Ave. Year: 2019; Ave. Citations: 38.9
The texts collectively explore the integration of sustainability and circular economy principles into supply chain operations and business practices. They highlight the challenges faced in implementing sustainable practices across multi-tier supply chains, such as cost, knowledge gaps, and supply chain complexity, and discuss strategies to overcome these barriers, including collaboration, innovation diffusion, supply chain mapping, performance measurement, and capacity building. The texts also examine the development of national circular economy indicators, the assessment of Industry 4.0 technologies from a sustainability perspective, and the role of green innovation in achieving sustainable performance in industries like the Spanish wine sector. These studies contribute to the growing body of research on sustainable supply chain management and circular economy practices, providing insights and recommendations for businesses and policymakers to drive the transition towards a more sustainable and circular economy.
